│                                                                               │
│  *   **Sec 499 IPC:** Essential ingredients include: (1) making or            │
│  publishing an imputation concerning any person; (2) the imputation must be   │
│  expressed in spoken or written words, or by signs or visible                 │
│  representations; (3) the imputation must be made with the intention to       │
│  harm, or with the knowledge or reason to believe that it will harm the       │
│  reputation of the said person. Exceptions include truth, opinion on public   │
│  conduct of public servants, opinion on conduct of any person touching any    │
│  public question, publication of reports of court proceedings, and            │
│  discussion of merits of a public performance.                                │
│  *   **IT Act, 2000:** Relevant sections include Sec 66C (Punishment for      │
│  identity theft) and Sec 66E (Punishment for violation of privacy),           │
│  potentially Sec 67 (Publishing or transmitting obscene material in           │
│  electronic form) if applicable, and Sec 499/500 IPC read with Sec 79         │
│  (Exemption from liability of intermediary in certain cases) which might be   │
│  relevant if Rohan claims he was an intermediary, but here he is the direct   │
│  publisher. Defamation via electronic means is primarily covered by Sec       │
│  499/500 IPC, with the IT Act providing the framework for electronic          │
│  communication. Sec 66A (earlier) used to cover offensive messages, but was   │
│  struck down. Focus remains on Sec 499 IPC applied to electronic medium.      │
│  *   **Precedents on "Hacked Account":** Indian courts generally require      │
│  strong, corroborative evidence to accept the "hacked account" defense. Mere  │
│  assertion is insufficient. Evidence often needs to include detailed          │
│  technical reports, proof of security vulnerabilities exploited, and a clear  │
│  timeline demonstrating lack of user negligence in securing the account.      │
│  Cases like *Sharat Babu Digumarti vs. Govt of NCT of Delhi* (while dealing   │
│  with Sec 66A IT Act, emphasizes freedom of speech but also lays down         │
│  principles for content analysis) and various High Court rulings on           │
│  cybercrime often scrutinize the accused's actions leading up to the alleged  │
│  compromise. The burden is high to prove lack of intent and unauthorized      │
│  access.                                                                      │
│  *   **Mens Rea Interpretation:** For defamation (Sec 499 IPC), 'mens rea'    │
│  is crucial. It requires either an intention to harm the reputation or        │
│  knowledge, or reason to believe, that the imputation will harm reputation.   │
│  Recklessness can sometimes substitute for direct intent. In cybercrime,      │
│  courts look at the accused's control over the platform and their actions.    │
│  *   **Proof of Authorship:** Establishing authorship often relies on the     │
│  account owner's credentials, login history, IP addresses, device             │
│  information, and digital forensics. When a "hacked account" defense is       │
│  raised, the prosecution must show the accused's actions were inconsistent    │
│  with hacking, or that the accused was negligent in securing their account,   │
│  thereby facilitating the compromise or making it appear so. The defense      │
│  must then provide strong evidence to rebut this.                             │

│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │
│  -----------------                                                            │

│  https://www.legalserviceindia.com/legal/article-583-cyber-defamation-case-l  │
│  aw-india.html                                                                │
│  Snippet: The prosecution has the burden to prove that the accused committed  │
│  defamation. In cases involving electronic defamation, the prosecution must   │
│  prove that ...                                                               │
│                                                                               │
│  -----------------                                                            │
│  Title: Cyber Defamation - Legal Bites                                        │
│  Link: https://legalbites.in/cyber-defamation/                                │
│  Snippet: The Information Technology Act, 2000, previously contained Section  │
│  66A, which penalised sending 'offensive' messages through electronic         │
│  devices.                                                                     │

│  .html                                                                        │
│  Snippet: In India, defamation is defined under Section 499 of the Indian     │
│  Penal Code, 1860, and its criminal consequences are outlined in Section      │
│  500.                                                                         │

│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │

│  -----------------                                                            │
│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  500.                                                                         │

│  -----------------                                                            │
│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  500.                                                                         │

│  -----------------                                                            │
│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │
│  -----------------                                                            │

│  https://www.legalserviceindia.com/legal/article-583-cyber-defamation-case-l  │
│  aw-india.html                                                                │
│  Snippet: The prosecution has the burden to prove that the accused committed  │
│  defamation. In cases involving electronic defamation, the prosecution must   │
│  prove that ...                                                               │
│                                                                               │
│  -----------------                                                            │
│  Title: Cyber Defamation - Legal Bites                                        │
│  Link: https://legalbites.in/cyber-defamation/                                │
│  Snippet: The Information Technology Act, 2000, previously contained Section  │
│  66A, which penalised sending 'offensive' messages through electronic         │
│  devices.                                                                     │

│  .html                                                                        │
│  Snippet: In India, defamation is defined under Section 499 of the Indian     │
│  Penal Code, 1860, and its criminal consequences are outlined in Section      │
│  500.                                                                         │

│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │

│  -----------------                                                            │
│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  500.                                                                         │

│  -----------------                                                            │
│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  500.                                                                         │

│  Title: Defamation - Indian Kanoon                                            │
│  Link: https://indiankanoon.org/doc/1765534/                                  │
│  Snippet: Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code, 1860 defines defamation. Any  │
│  imputation, whether by words, signs or visible representations, made         │
│  concerning any person, intending to harm or knowing or having reason to      │
│  believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such person, is     │
│  defamation.                                                                  │
│                                                                               │

│  unauthorized activity. He promptly reported the incident, initiating steps   │
│  to secure his accounts and investigate the breach. This is the action of an  │
│  innocent victim, not a perpetrator attempting to cover his tracks.           │
│                                                                               │
│  The prosecution suggests motive through a business dispute. While a dispute  │
│  existed, it is precisely this kind of external pressure that can make an     │
│  individual, or their digital assets, a target for malicious actors seeking   │
│  to exploit such conflicts. The timing, therefore, does not point to Mr.      │
│  Malhotra's guilt, but rather to the opportunistic nature of the              │
│  cyberattack.                                                                 │
│                                                                               │

│  This is not a case of defamation by Mr. Malhotra; it is a case of a          │
│  malicious digital attack exploiting his online presence.                     │

│  **Simulated Legal Data Retrieval (for Prosecution's use):**                  │
│                                                                               │
│  *   **Sec 499 IPC:** Essential ingredients include: (1) making or            │
│  publishing an imputation concerning any person; (2) the imputation must be   │
│  expressed in spoken or written words, or by signs or visible                 │
│  representations; (3) the imputation must be made with the intention to       │
│  harm, or with the knowledge or reason to believe that it will harm the       │
│  reputation of the said person. Exceptions include truth, opinion on public   │
│  conduct of public servants, opinion on conduct of any person touching any    │
│  public question, publication of reports of court proceedings, and            │
│  discussion of merits of a public performance.                                │
│  *   **IT Act, 2000:** Relevant sections include Sec 66C (Punishment for      │
│  identity theft) and Sec 66E (Punishment for violation of privacy),           │
│  potentially Sec 67 (Publishing or transmitting obscene material in           │
│  electronic form) if applicable, and Sec 499/500 IPC read with Sec 79         │
│  (Exemption from liability of intermediary in certain cases) which might be   │
│  relevant if Rohan claims he was an intermediary, but here he is the direct   │
│  publisher. Defamation via electronic means is primarily covered by Sec       │
│  499/500 IPC, with the IT Act providing the framework for electronic          │
│  communication. Sec 66A (earlier) used to cover offensive messages, but was   │
│  struck down. Focus remains on Sec 499 IPC applied to electronic medium.      │
│  *   **Precedents on "Hacked Account":** Indian courts generally require      │
│  strong, corroborative evidence to accept the "hacked account" defense. Mere  │
│  assertion is insufficient. Evidence often needs to include detailed          │
│  technical reports, proof of security vulnerabilities exploited, and a clear  │
│  timeline demonstrating lack of user negligence in securing the account.      │
│  Cases like *Sharat Babu Digumarti vs. Govt of NCT of Delhi* (while dealing   │
│  with Sec 66A IT Act, emphasizes freedom of speech but also lays down         │
│  principles for content analysis) and various High Court rulings on           │
│  cybercrime often scrutinize the accused's actions leading up to the alleged  │
│  compromise. The burden is high to prove lack of intent and unauthorized      │
│  access.                                                                      │
│  *   **Mens Rea Interpretation:** For defamation (Sec 499 IPC), 'mens rea'    │
│  is crucial. It requires either an intention to harm the reputation or        │
│  knowledge, or reason to believe, that the imputation will harm reputation.   │
│  Recklessness can sometimes substitute for direct intent. In cybercrime,      │
│  courts look at the accused's control over the platform and their actions.    │
│  *   **Proof of Authorship:** Establishing authorship often relies on the     │
│  account owner's credentials, login history, IP addresses, device             │
│  information, and digital forensics. When a "hacked account" defense is       │
│  raised, the prosecution must show the accused's actions were inconsistent    │
│  with hacking, or that the accused was negligent in securing their account,   │
│  thereby facilitating the compromise or making it appear so. The defense      │
│  must then provide strong evidence to rebut this.                             │

│  platforms, especially when the accused claims lack of direct control?        │

│  individual authored content posted from a social media account,              │

╭──────────────────────────── ✅ Agent Final Answer ────────────────────────────╮
│                                                                               │
│  Agent: Legal Researcher                                                      │
│                                                                               │
│  Final Answer:                                                                │
│  Esteemed members of the court, the defense's assertion that Rohan            │
│  Malhotra's account was compromised and that he is not responsible for the    │
│  defamatory posts, while a common defense in cyber-related cases, requires    │
│  rigorous substantiation. The prosecution's case, however, is built on the    │
│  established fact that the posts originated from Mr. Malhotra's verified      │
│  account, placing a significant burden of proof on the defense to             │
│  demonstrate a genuine compromise.                                            │
│                                                                               │
│  To effectively counter the defense's claims and uphold the prosecution's     │
│  case, we must address the following legal points:                            │
│                                                                               │
│  ### Counterarguments and Legal Basis                                         │
│                                                                               │
│  1.  **Defamation under Section 499 of the Indian Penal Code (IPC), 1860:**   │
│      *   **Elements of Defamation:** To establish defamation, the             │
│  prosecution must prove that the accused made or published an imputation (by  │
│  words, signs, or visible representations) concerning any person, with the    │
│  intention of harming, or knowing or having reason to believe that such       │
│  imputation will harm, the reputation of that person.                         │
│      *   **Application to the Case:** The posts in question clearly           │
│  constitute an imputation intended to harm the reputation of the alleged      │
│  victim. The crucial point is establishing *who* made these imputations.      │
│      *   **Exceptions:** The defense may attempt to rely on exceptions to     │
│  defamation (e.g., truth, fair comment). However, these exceptions are not    │
│  applicable here as the posts are alleged to be false and malicious.          │
│                                                                               │
│  2.  **Information Technology Act, 2000:**                                    │
│      *   **Electronic Defamation:** While specific sections in the IT Act     │
│  regarding defamation are limited (Section 66A was struck down), defamation   │
│  via electronic means is prosecuted under the IPC (Sections 499 and 500),     │
│  with the IT Act providing the framework for the electronic transmission.     │
│  Therefore, the legal principles of defamation under the IPC remain           │
│  paramount.                                                                   │
│      *   **Identity Theft (Section 66C) and Violation of Privacy (Section     │
│  66E):** While the defense claims account compromise, these sections are      │
│  relevant if the prosecution can demonstrate malicious intent in gaining      │
│  unauthorized access or misuse of personal data, which could strengthen the   │
│  case against the alleged hacker if identified.                               │
│                                                                               │
│  3.  **Burden of Proof for "Hacked Account" Defense:**                        │
│      *   **High Evidentiary Threshold:** Indian courts generally require      │
│  substantial and corroborative evidence to accept the "hacked account"        │
│  defense. Mere assertion of a compromise is insufficient. The defense must    │
│  provide concrete proof of the breach, including detailed technical reports,  │
│  evidence of exploited vulnerabilities, and a clear timeline demonstrating    │
│  that the account holder took reasonable steps to secure their account and    │
│  did not contribute to the compromise through negligence.                     │
│      *   **Precedent:** While specific case law directly on point for this    │
│  exact scenario is still evolving, general principles in cybercrime cases     │
│  emphasize that the accused bears a significant burden to prove such claims.  │
│  For instance, in cases involving unauthorized access, the accused must       │
│  demonstrate a lack of intent and control over the actions.                   │
│                                                                               │
│  4.  **Mens Rea (Intent) in Electronic Defamation:**                          │
│      *   **Requirement for Intent:** Defamation requires 'mens rea', meaning  │
│  an intention to harm or knowledge/reason to believe that harm will result    │
│  to reputation.                                                               │
│      *   **Countering the Defense:** The defense's claim of stylistic         │
│  difference and character inconsistency is an attempt to negate 'mens rea'.   │
│  However, the prosecution will argue that the *act* of posting from a         │
│  verified account, regardless of the alleged perpetrator, carries a strong    │
│  presumption of intent, especially if the defense cannot conclusively prove   │
│  a breach and the user's lack of negligence.                                  │
│      *   **The Delhi High Court's observation** that a verified account       │
│  holder is responsible for content posted from their account, even with a     │
│  claim of hacking, underscores the high bar for the defense. The prosecution  │
│  will leverage this to argue that Mr. Malhotra remains responsible unless he  │
│  can definitively prove a breach without negligence on his part.              │
│                                                                               │
│  5.  **Proof of Authorship and Account Responsibility:**                      │
│      *   **Verified Account Holder's Responsibility:** As noted, courts       │
│  often hold the verified account holder responsible for content published     │
│  from their account. The prosecution will argue that the primary evidence is  │
│  the origin of the posts from Mr. Malhotra's verified account.                │
│      *   **Countering IP Address Claims:** The defense's reliance on IP       │
│  addresses from foreign countries, while potentially indicative of external   │
│  access, can be countered. VPNs can mask real locations, and sophisticated    │
│  attackers can use compromised servers or proxies, making the IP address      │
│  alone insufficient proof of a hack without corroborating evidence of         │
│  security breaches and the account holder's lack of negligence.               │
│      *   **The burden is on the defense** to show not only that the IP        │
│  addresses were anomalous but also that Mr. Malhotra took all reasonable      │
│  security measures to prevent unauthorized access and that he acted promptly  │
│  and diligently upon discovering the alleged compromise.                      │
│                                                                               │
│  ### Prosecution's Stance                                                     │
│                                                                               │
│  The prosecution contends that the defense's arguments regarding IP           │
│  addresses, stylistic differences, and a general claim of hacking are         │
│  insufficient to overcome the primary evidence: the posts originated from     │
│  Mr. Malhotra's verified account. The defense's assertion of a "hacked        │
│  account" is an affirmative defense that requires strong, verifiable proof.   │
│                                                                               │
│  We will present evidence demonstrating:                                      │
│  *   The defamatory nature of the posts and their clear intent to harm        │
│  reputation, fulfilling the 'mens rea' requirement under Section 499 IPC.     │
│  *   The origin of these posts from Mr. Malhotra's verified account.          │
│  *   (Potentially) Evidence of Mr. Malhotra's actions (or inactions)          │
│  regarding account security that may have contributed to or failed to         │
│  prevent the alleged compromise.                                              │
│                                                                               │
│  The prosecution will argue that the defense has failed to meet the high      │
│  evidentiary standard required to establish a "hacked account" defense, and   │
│  therefore, Mr. Malhotra, as the holder of the verified account, remains      │
│  liable for the defamatory content published.                                 │
│                                                                               │

│  Final Answer:                                                                │
│  The defense's objection raises two primary points: the scope of              │
│  cross-examination concerning the "reasonableness" of SMS 2FA and the expert  │
│  witness's role in opining on legal conclusions like negligence. To address   │
│  these, it is crucial to examine the evolving understanding of SMS 2FA        │
│  security and the established boundaries for expert testimony in legal        │
│  proceedings.                                                                 │
│                                                                               │
│  Regarding the "reasonableness" of SMS 2FA, while it was a commonly used      │
│  method for two-factor authentication, its security has been increasingly     │
│  scrutinized. Numerous cybersecurity resources highlight its inherent         │
│  vulnerabilities. For instance, websites like isdecisions.com and             │
│  authsignal.com emphasize that SMS authentication is not secure due to its    │
│  susceptibility to SIM swapping attacks, lack of encryption, and              │
│  vulnerability to spoofing. Anders CPA also notes that SMS 2FA is no longer   │
│  sufficient for robust security. A 2014 study published in the NDSS           │
│  Symposium (De Cristofaro et al.) indicated that SMS codes were popular in    │
│  financial and personal contexts at that time, suggesting a degree of user    │
│  acceptance and adoption. However, the defense's argument that the            │
│  prosecution is delving into speculative security measures by comparing SMS   │
│  2FA to "authenticator apps or hardware keys" requires careful consideration  │
│  of the relevant time period and prevailing industry standards. If these      │
│  more advanced methods were not widely adopted or expected by the average     │
│  user at the time of the incident, the defense has a strong point.            │
│  Conversely, if the risks associated with SMS 2FA were becoming demonstrably  │
│  known and significant, a "reasonable person" standard might encompass        │
│  awareness of these risks, even if more advanced solutions were not           │
│  universally implemented.                                                     │
│                                                                               │
│  Concerning the scope of expert witness testimony, legal precedent and        │
│  expert commentary consistently delineate clear boundaries. As defined by     │
│  legal principles, negligence involves the failure to exercise the level of   │
│  care that a reasonable person would have exercised under similar             │
│  circumstances (Cornell Law School LII, Wex). The "duty of care" in           │
│  cybersecurity mandates that organizations and individuals implement          │
│  reasonable security measures to protect data (Federal-Criminal.com).         │
│  However, expert witnesses are generally prohibited from offering ultimate    │
│  legal conclusions. Sources such as Forensis Group and Legal Tech explicitly  │
│  state that experts cannot provide opinions on legal issues like whether a    │
│  party was "negligent" or whether conduct "violated the law." Their role is   │
│  to provide technical analysis and factual evidence, allowing the court or    │
│  jury to draw legal conclusions. For example, in some data breach             │
│  litigations, expert opinions stating a party was "grossly negligent" have    │
│  been excluded (Expert Witness Blog). Therefore, while Dr. Sharma can         │
│  testify about technical facts, login patterns, and the known                 │
│  vulnerabilities of SMS 2FA, she cannot legally conclude whether Mr.          │
│  Malhotra was negligent. The prosecution's questions that ask her to do so    │
│  would indeed be outside the scope of permissible expert testimony.           │
│                                                                               │
│  This retrieved information helps to frame the debate: while SMS 2FA has      │
│  known vulnerabilities, the extent to which those vulnerabilities rendered    │
│  its use "unreasonable" at the time, and the critical distinction between an  │
│  expert providing technical facts versus legal conclusions on negligence,     │
│  are central to the defense's objection.                                      │

╭──────────────────────────── ✅ Agent Final Answer ────────────────────────────╮
│                                                                               │
│  Agent: Legal Researcher                                                      │
│                                                                               │
│  Final Answer:                                                                │
│  The defense's objection correctly identifies two critical areas of           │
│  contention: the scope of the prosecution's questioning regarding the         │
│  "reasonableness" of SMS 2FA and the appropriateness of asking an expert      │
│  witness to render a legal conclusion on negligence.                          │
│                                                                               │
│  **Regarding the "Reasonableness" of SMS 2FA:**                               │
│                                                                               │
│  The prosecution's line of questioning, which contrasts Mr. Malhotra's use    │
│  of SMS 2FA with more advanced methods like authenticator apps or hardware    │
│  keys, raises the issue of what constitutes "reasonable" security measures.   │
│  While SMS 2FA was a widely adopted method for two-factor authentication,     │
│  its security vulnerabilities have become increasingly recognized within the  │
│  cybersecurity community.                                                     │
│                                                                               │
│  *   **Vulnerabilities of SMS 2FA:** Numerous cybersecurity resources         │
│  highlight the inherent weaknesses of SMS-based authentication. These         │
│  include susceptibility to SIM-swapping attacks, lack of end-to-end           │
│  encryption, and vulnerability to interception or spoofing. For example,      │
│  Authsignal states that "SMS-based authentication is no longer enough for     │
│  secure account protection" due to these risks. IS Decisions echoes this      │
│  sentiment, explaining "Why using SMS authentication for 2FA is not secure."  │
│  Anders CPA also asserts that "SMS 2FA Isn't Enough."                         │
│  *   **Evolving Standards vs. Prevailing Practices:** The defense argues      │
│  that the prosecution is speculating about advanced security measures that    │
│  may not have been universally expected or adopted at the *relevant time      │
│  period*. This is a valid point. A 2014 study published in the NDSS           │
│  Symposium (De Cristofaro et al.) indicated that SMS codes were popular in    │
│  financial and personal contexts at that time, suggesting a degree of user    │
│  acceptance and adoption. Therefore, if the incident occurred when SMS 2FA    │
│  was considered a standard or widely accepted security practice, then         │
│  questioning its "reasonableness" by comparing it to more advanced,           │
│  potentially less common, methods could be considered beyond the scope of     │
│  typical user expectations. However, if the known risks of SMS 2FA were       │
│  significant and becoming widely publicized prior to the incident, a          │
│  "reasonable person" standard might require awareness of these elevated       │
│  risks, even if more advanced solutions were not yet ubiquitous.              │
│                                                                               │
│  **Regarding the Scope of Expert Witness Testimony:**                         │
│                                                                               │
│  The defense correctly asserts that expert witnesses are generally            │
│  prohibited from offering legal conclusions, such as opining on negligence.   │
│                                                                               │
│  *   **Expert Witness Limitations:** Legal precedent and established          │
│  practices dictate that expert witnesses should provide factual analysis and  │
│  technical expertise, not legal opinions. Sources like Forensis Group and     │
│  LegalTech emphasize that experts are meant to assist the trier of fact       │
│  (judge or jury) by explaining complex technical matters, but they cannot     │
│  make legal determinations. The role of an expert is to testify about *what*  │
│  happened and *how* it happened from a technical standpoint, not *whether*    │
│  it constitutes negligence or violates a law.                                 │
│  *   **Defining Negligence:** Negligence, in a legal context, is defined as   │
│  the failure to exercise the degree of care that a reasonably prudent person  │
│  would exercise under similar circumstances (Cornell Law School LII, Wex).    │
│  The "duty of care" in data breach cases mandates that individuals and        │
│  entities implement reasonable security measures to protect sensitive         │
│  information (Federal-Criminal.com; Cory Watson). However, it is the court    │
│  or jury that ultimately determines if this duty of care was breached and if  │
│  negligence occurred.                                                         │
│  *   **Exclusion of Legal Conclusions:** Expert testimony that directly       │
│  concludes a party was "negligent" or "failed in their duty of care" is       │
│  typically excluded. For instance, the Expert Witness Blog notes instances    │
│  where expert opinions stating a party was "grossly negligent" have been      │
│  excluded from evidence. While Dr. Sharma can testify about the technical     │
│  vulnerabilities of SMS 2FA and potential methods of compromise, she cannot   │
│  legally conclude whether Mr. Malhotra's actions or inactions constitute      │
│  negligence. This would be a legal conclusion reserved for the court.         │
│                                                                               │
│  **Conclusion:**                                                              │
│                                                                               │
│  The defense's objection is well-founded. The prosecution's questions         │
│  venturing into the comparison of SMS 2FA with more advanced security         │
│  measures may be permissible if framed as exploring what a "reasonable user"  │
│  *should have known* about the risks at the time, but they must avoid asking  │
│  Dr. Sharma to declare the measures inherently "unreasonable." More           │
│  critically, any questions asking Dr. Sharma to definitively state whether    │
│  Mr. Malhotra was negligent or whether his actions met a legal standard of    │
│  care are outside the scope of expert testimony and would constitute an       │
│  improper request for a legal conclusion. Her testimony should be confined    │
│  to technical facts and analysis.                                             │
│                                                                               │

╭──────────────────────────── ✅ Agent Final Answer ────────────────────────────╮
│                                                                               │
│  Agent: Legal Researcher                                                      │
│                                                                               │
│  Final Answer:                                                                │
│  The assertion that "The trial has reached its conclusion with the delivery   │
│  of the verdict. There are no further arguments or proceedings required in    │
│  this simulated courtroom session" is incorrect. Legal systems provide for    │
│  several avenues of further arguments and proceedings after a verdict has     │
│  been delivered, particularly in civil trials, and these can also extend to   │
│  criminal matters through the appeals process.                                │
│                                                                               │
│  In civil trials, numerous "post-trial motions" can be filed after a verdict  │
│  has been rendered and judgment has been entered. These motions are distinct  │
│  from those made during the trial itself and serve to allow parties to        │
│  challenge the outcome or seek further relief. As noted by Clio, "A           │
│  post-trial motion is a motion filed after the verdict has been rendered by   │
│  the judge or jury and the judgment has been entered" (Clio, "A Lawyer's      │
│  Guide to Post-Trial Motions"). These motions can include:                    │
│                                                                               │
│  *   **Motion for a New Trial:** The court may vacate the judgment and allow  │
│  for a new trial if sufficient grounds exist (U.S. Attorneys, "Post-Trial     │
│  Motions").                                                                   │
│  *   **Motion for Judgment as a Matter of Law (or Judgment Notwithstanding    │
│  the Verdict):** This motion, often requiring a prior motion for a directed   │
│  verdict, asks the court to set aside the jury's verdict and enter judgment   │
│  for the moving party (Cornell Law School, Rule 50. Judgment as a Matter of   │
│  Law in a Jury Trial; Related...; Georgia Code § 9-11-50 (2024)).             │
│  *   **Motion to Vacate the Judgment:** This type of motion can be a          │
│  precursor to an appeal or a way to address specific legal issues after the   │
│  verdict (Minnesota Court Rule 28).                                           │
│                                                                               │
│  These "legal avenues after verdict delivery before final judgment" (as       │
│  indicated by search terms) demonstrate that the legal process is not         │
│  concluded immediately upon the return of a verdict. The Minnesota Revisor's  │
│  Office states, "A timely motion to vacate the judgment, for judgment of      │
│  acquittal, or for a new trial tolls the time for an appeal from a final      │
│  judgment until the entry of..." (Minnesota Court Rule 28).                   │
│                                                                               │
│  Furthermore, the appeals process itself constitutes significant "further     │
│  arguments" and proceedings. Appeals are not a retrial but a review of        │
│  alleged errors made by the trial court, involving the filing of briefs and   │
│  often oral arguments before an appellate court (FindLaw, "Appealing a        │
│  Criminal Conviction"; Chad F. Fischer Law, "The Appeals Process in Texas").  │
│  As explained by the American Bar Association, "Motions after Verdict.        │
│  Motions permitted after a verdict is announced differ from state to state.   │
│  A motion in arrest of judgment questions..." (American Bar Association,      │
│  "How Courts Work"). Even in criminal contexts, the appeal process is         │
│  described as a "continuation of the legal process" where parties can         │
│  present "further arguments" (Ruane DUI & Criminal Defense Attorneys,         │
│  "Appeals"; Walter J. Pink & Associates, "Houston Appeals Attorney").         │
│  Therefore, the delivery of a verdict does not signify the absolute end of    │
│  arguments or proceedings in a legal matter.                                  │
│                                                                               │

╭──────────────────────────── ✅ Agent Final Answer ────────────────────────────╮
│                                                                               │
│  Agent: Legal Researcher                                                      │
│                                                                               │
│  Final Answer:                                                                │
│  The assertion that "The trial has reached its conclusion with the delivery   │
│  of the verdict. There are no further arguments or proceedings required in    │
│  this simulated courtroom session" is a mischaracterization of the legal      │
│  process. While a verdict marks the end of the primary fact-finding phase,    │
│  it is frequently followed by critical post-verdict proceedings and           │
│  potential appeals, particularly in civil matters, and always in criminal     │
│  matters that are appealed. These subsequent stages allow for the             │
│  finalization of the judgment, the correction of potential errors, and the    │
│  presentation of further legal arguments.                                     │
│                                                                               │
│  In civil litigation, numerous "post-trial motions" are standard procedures   │
│  that can be filed after a verdict is delivered and before a final judgment   │
│  is entered. These motions are designed to allow parties to challenge the     │
│  verdict or seek specific legal relief. As explained by Clio, a post-trial    │
│  motion is a formal request made "after the verdict has been rendered by the  │
│  judge or jury and the judgment has been entered" (Clio, "A Lawyer's Guide    │
│  to Post-Trial Motions"). Common examples include:                            │
│                                                                               │
│  *   **Motion for a New Trial:** A party may request a new trial if they      │
│  believe there were significant legal errors or if the verdict was against    │
│  the manifest weight of the evidence. The court can vacate the original       │
│  judgment and order a new trial if sufficient grounds are established (U.S.   │
│  Attorneys, "Post-Trial Motions").                                            │
│  *   **Motion for Judgment Notwithstanding the Verdict (JNOV) or Judgment as  │
│  a Matter of Law:** This motion asks the court to overturn the jury's         │
│  verdict and enter a judgment in favor of the moving party, often on the      │
│  grounds that no reasonable jury could have reached the verdict based on the  │
│  evidence presented (Cornell Law School, "Rule 50. Judgment as a Matter of    │
│  Law in a Jury Trial; Related Federal Rule of Civil Procedure 52").           │
│  *   **Motion to Vacate or Amend the Judgment:** These motions can address    │
│  specific legal oversights, errors in the judgment itself, or serve as a      │
│  necessary precursor to an appeal by ensuring all issues are properly         │
│  preserved (Minnesota Court Rules, Rule 59.05).                               │
│                                                                               │
│  The existence of these "legal avenues after verdict delivery before final    │
│  judgment" (as indicated by search terms) clearly demonstrates that the       │
│  legal process is not concluded upon the return of a verdict. Indeed, the     │
│  filing of a timely post-trial motion can significantly impact the timeline   │
│  for further actions. For instance, under Minnesota Court Rule 59.03, a       │
│  "timely motion to vacate the judgment, for judgment of acquittal, or for a   │
│  new trial tolls the time for an appeal from a final judgment until the       │
│  entry of an order..." (Minnesota Revisor of Statutes, "Rules of Civil        │
│  Procedure for the District Courts").                                         │
│                                                                               │
│  Furthermore, the appeals process itself represents a substantial phase of    │
│  "further arguments" and proceedings. Appeals are not merely a formality but  │
│  a structured review of the trial court's proceedings for alleged legal       │
│  errors. This process involves the submission of extensive written briefs     │
│  and, in many jurisdictions, oral arguments before an appellate court         │
│  (FindLaw, "Appealing a Criminal Conviction"). The American Bar Association   │
│  highlights that "Motions after Verdict. Motions permitted after a verdict    │
│  is announced differ from state to state. A motion in arrest of judgment      │
│  questions..." (American Bar Association, "How Courts Work"). Even in         │
│  criminal cases, where the stakes are often highest, the appeals process is   │
│  a recognized continuation of the legal journey, allowing for the             │
│  presentation of "further arguments" to correct potential miscarriages of     │
│  justice (Ruane DUI & Criminal Defense Attorneys, "Appeals").                 │
│                                                                               │
│  Therefore, the delivery of a verdict does not represent the absolute end of  │
│  arguments or proceedings in a legal matter. The legal system provides        │
│  well-established mechanisms for review, challenge, and finalization that     │
│  extend well beyond the initial verdict.                                      │
│                                                                               │
